alt=\"\"/>","descriptionType":"html","publishedDate":"Tue, 08 Jun 2021 01:34:02 +0000","feedId":17,"bgimg":"https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2018/10/figuring_jacket_final.jpg","linkMd5":"e401792ab2fdd3535b3ecbc78f230cdc","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx29/cdn68@2020_3/2021/07/12/18-35-26-104_dcc9d82e9f830223.webp","destWidth":1200,"destHeight":1824,"sourceBytes":98450,"destBytes":98450,"author":"Maria Popova","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2018/10/figuring_jacket_final.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx29/cdn68@2020_3/2021/07/12/18-35-26-104_dcc9d82e9f830223.webp","https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2017/02/rachelcarson_undersea.jpg?resize=680%2C398&ssl=1":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx6/cdn27@2020_6/2021/07/12/18-35-33-667_b3e50e32670dfd50.webp","https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/hasuikawase1.jpg?resize=680%2C1014&ssl=1":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx15/cdn48@2020_3/2021/07/12/18-35-35-061_3304ec43464b355d.webp","https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/06/greatwave_hokusai.jpg?resize=680%2C457&ssl=1":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx14/cdn72@2020_4/2021/07/12/18-35-35-187_526ec7cb657245bd.webp","https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/06/yaggi_nature_sm.jpg?resize=680%2C460&ssl=1":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx25/cdn95@2020_4/2021/07/12/18-35-34-568_d3e21adefb27b643.webp","https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2017/08/rachelcarson_1951.jpg?resize=680%2C338&ssl=1":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx19/cdn67@2020_5/2021/07/12/18-35-33-819_05813d21c01c2a83.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/brainpickings/rss/~4/fSkeIJh0ySE":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx2/cdn68@2020_1/2021/07/12/18-35-33-989_a952d71376a2040f.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1626114913561},{"createdTime":"2021-07-13 02:35:13","updatedTime":"2021-07-13 02:35:13","title":"Sarah Mapps Douglass’s Flowers: The First Surviving Art Signed by an African-American Woman","link":"https://www.brainpickings.org/?p=73685","description":"<h3>A rose is a rose is a revolution. </h3>\n<hr>\n<p>In his thrice-revised and expanded autobiographies, <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/tag/frederick-douglass/\">Frederick Douglass</a>, born Frederick Bailey, recounts changing his surname multiple times to cover his fugitive trail. When the time came to settle on a permanent name, he invited the man in whose home was taking refuge &#8212; a free black man devoted to helping fugitive slaves &#8212; to choose for him, as a token of gratitude. His host suggested Douglas &#8212; the self-possessed Highlander hero of one of the era&#8217;s greatest literary blockbusters, Sir Walter Scott&#8217;s poem <em>The Lady of the Lake</em>. In his retelling, Frederick Douglass brushes past the additional <em>s</em>, with the vague intimation that he added it for distinction. </p>\n<p>But there is another probable possibility for the peculiar spelling. Here was an orphan with no sense of roots, an aching ambivalence about his parentage, and a longing for communal belonging. And here was the grown man of genius retelling his own story of becoming, aware &#8212; like all persons of genius &#8212; that a larger mythos of colossal cultural significance hangs upon their private myth. </p>\n<figure id=\"attachment_73686\"  class=\"wp-caption aligncenter\"><img loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/06/sarahmappsdouglass_forgetmenot1.jpg?resize=680%2C872&#038;ssl=1\" alt=\"\" width=\"680\" height=\"872\" class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-73694\" srcset=\"https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/06/sarahmappsdouglass_forgetmenot1.jpg?w=745&#38;ssl=1 745w, https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/06/sarahmappsdouglass_forgetmenot1.jpg?resize=320%2C410&#38;ssl=1 320w, https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/06/sarahmappsdouglass_forgetmenot1.jpg?resize=600%2C769&#38;ssl=1 600w, https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/06/sarahmappsdouglass_forgetmenot1.jpg?resize=240%2C308&#38;ssl=1 240w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 680px) 100vw, 680px\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /><figcaption class=\"wp-caption-text\"><em>Forget me not</em> by Sarah Mapps Douglass</figcaption></figure>\n<p>Since the dawn of the abolition movement, women had played an active and ardent role in fundraising, organizing, and public advocacy &#8212; women who risked ostracism, or worse, for exercising the agency of stepping outside the narrow confines of the domestic sphere allotted them and into the cosmos of political activism. One of the movement&#8217;s leading women was the young Philadelphian <strong>Sarah Mapps Douglass</strong> (September 9, 1806&#8211;September 8, 1882), who at only twenty-five had organized a major fundraising campaign for the primary journalistic instrument of abolition &#8212; William Lloyd Garrison’s paper <em>The Liberator</em>, on the pages of which Frederick Douglass found and trained his own literary voice. </p>\n<p>The daughter of two active abolitionists, Sarah was born into one of America’s most prominent black families and raised in the Quaker tradition &#8212; her grandfather, Cyrus, had been a slave to a local Quaker baker who liberated him and taught him the baking trade; Cyrus soon opened his own successful bakery, which allowed him to fund and operate a school for black children from his home, later becoming a founding member of the pioneering Free African Society seventy-five years before Abraham Lincoln <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2019/11/04/abraham-lincoln-equality-exclusion/\">staked his credo and his life</a> on the Emancipation Proclamation. </p>\n<figure id=\"attachment_73688\"  class=\"wp-caption aligncenter\"><a href=\"https://society6.com/product/rose-by-sarah-mapps-douglass-1833_print?curator=brainpicker\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"><img loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/06/sarahmappsdouglass_rose.jpg?resize=680%2C860&#038;ssl=1\" alt=\"\" width=\"680\" height=\"860\" class=\"size-full wp-image-73688\" srcset=\"https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/06/sarahmappsdouglass_rose.jpg?w=1200&#38;ssl=1 1200w, https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/06/sarahmappsdouglass_rose.jpg?resize=320%2C405&#38;ssl=1 320w, https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/06/sarahmappsdouglass_rose.jpg?resize=600%2C759&#38;ssl=1 600w, https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/06/sarahmappsdouglass_rose.jpg?resize=240%2C304&#38;ssl=1 240w, https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/06/sarahmappsdouglass_rose.jpg?resize=768%2C972&#38;ssl=1 768w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 680px) 100vw, 680px\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></a><figcaption class=\"wp-caption-text\">Rose painting and poem by Sarah Mapps Douglass (<a href=\"http://librarycompany.org\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">Library Company of Philadelphia</a>. Available as <a href=\"https://society6.com/product/rose-by-sarah-mapps-douglass-1833_print?curator=brainpicker\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">a print</a> and as <a href=\"https://society6.com/brainpicker/collection/vintage-science-face-masks?curator=brainpicker\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">a face mask</a>.)</figcaption></figure>\n<p>Sarah was nursed on a love of literature and a love of nature, immersed in art and music, and lavished with painting lessons in a bubble of her privilege &#8212; <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2015/09/14/margo-jefferson-negroland-privilege/\">provisional and relative</a>, as all privilege is. Growing up amid the intellectual ferment of abolition, she grasped the terrors of slavery only abstractly, as an idea and not a felt reality, not fully imagining how other children who looked like her lived very different lives, how people who looked like her parents died gruesome deaths. Then, as a young woman, she performed one of those rare, triumphal acts that signify true maturity and grandeur of spirit: changing one&#8217;s mind as it expands to take in a broader perspective and publicly acknowledging one&#8217;s previously limited views. </p>\n<figure id=\"attachment_73691\"  class=\"wp-caption aligncenter\"><a href=\"https://society6.com/product/flowers-and-scroll-1833_print?curator=brainpicker\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"><img loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/06/rose_scroll.jpg?resize=680%2C835&#038;ssl=1\" alt=\"\" width=\"680\" height=\"835\" class=\"size-full wp-image-73691\" srcset=\"https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/06/rose_scroll.jpg?w=1200&#38;ssl=1 1200w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/06/rose_scroll.jpg?resize=320%2C393&#38;ssl=1 320w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/06/rose_scroll.jpg?resize=600%2C737&#38;ssl=1 600w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/06/rose_scroll.jpg?resize=240%2C295&#38;ssl=1 240w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/06/rose_scroll.jpg?resize=768%2C943&#38;ssl=1 768w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 680px) 100vw, 680px\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></a><figcaption class=\"wp-caption-text\">Unsigned rose and scroll, likely by Sarah Mapps Douglass, from Amy Matilda Cassey&#8217;s friendship album. (<a href=\"http://librarycompany.org\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">Library Company of Philadelphia</a>. Available as <a href=\"https://society6.com/product/flowers-and-scroll-1833_print?curator=brainpicker\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">a print</a>, <a href=\"https://society6.com/brainpicker/collection/vintage-science-face-masks?curator=brainpicker\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">a face mask</a>, and <a href=\"https://society6.com/product/flowers-and-scroll-1833_cards?curator=brainpicker\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">stationery cards</a>.)</figcaption></figure>\n<p>In 1832, Sarah Mapps Douglass recounted her awakening to the urgency of abolition in a rousing speech at a gathering of the Female Literary Society of Philadelphia, of which she was a leader &#8212; the first specialty library for African-American women, literate and illiterate, free and enslaved, devoted to “the cultivation of intellectual powers” as the highest tribute to the sanctity of human nature. She told the women gathered before her:</p>\n<blockquote><p>An English writer has said, ‘We must feel deeply before we can act rightly; from that absorbing, heart-rendering compassion for ourselves springs a deeper sympathy for others, and from a sense of our weakness and our own upbraidings arises a disposition to be indulgent, to forbear, to forgive.’ This is my experience. One short year ago, how different were my feelings on the subject of slavery! It is true, the wail of the captive sometimes came to my ear in the midst of my happiness, and caused my heart to bleed for his wrongs; but, alas! the impression was as evanescent as the early cloud and morning dew. I had formed a little world of my own, and cared not to move beyond its precincts. But how was the scene changed when I beheld the oppressor lurking on the border of my own peaceful home! I saw his iron hand stretched forth to seize me as his prey, and the cause of the slave became my own.</p></blockquote>\n<p>The following year, in a friendship album compiled by her Philadelphian friend and fellow activist Amy Matilda Cassey, Sarah Mapps Douglass fused her twin loves of literature and nature with her artistic acumen in a series of consummate flowers she painted alongside selections from poems she transcribed in her lovely longhand. </p>\n<figure id=\"attachment_73690\"  class=\"wp-caption aligncenter\"><a href=\"https://society6.com/product/flowers-by-sarah-mapps-douglass-1833_print?curator=brainpicker\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"><img loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/06/sarahmappsdouglass_flower.jpg?resize=680%2C844&#038;ssl=1\" alt=\"\" width=\"680\" height=\"844\" class=\"size-full wp-image-73690\" srcset=\"https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/06/sarahmappsdouglass_flower.jpg?w=1200&#38;ssl=1 1200w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/06/sarahmappsdouglass_flower.jpg?resize=320%2C397&#38;ssl=1 320w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/06/sarahmappsdouglass_flower.jpg?resize=600%2C745&#38;ssl=1 600w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/06/sarahmappsdouglass_flower.jpg?resize=240%2C298&#38;ssl=1 240w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/06/sarahmappsdouglass_flower.jpg?resize=768%2C953&#38;ssl=1 768w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 680px) 100vw, 680px\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></a><figcaption class=\"wp-caption-text\">Flowers by Sarah Mapps Douglass, with transcribed anonymous poem. (<a href=\"http://librarycompany.org\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">Library Company of Philadelphia</a>. Available as <a href=\"https://society6.com/product/flowers-by-sarah-mapps-douglass-1833_print?curator=brainpicker\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">a print</a>, <a href=\"https://society6.com/brainpicker/collection/vintage-science-face-masks?curator=brainpicker\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">a face mask</a>, and <a href=\"https://society6.com/product/flowers-by-sarah-mapps-douglass-1833_cards?curator=brainpicker\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">stationery cards</a>.)</figcaption></figure>\n<blockquote><p>No marvel woman should love flowers, they bear<br />\nSo much of the fanciful similitude<br />\nTo her own history; like herself repaying<br />\nWith such sweet interest all the cherishing<br />\nThat calls their beauty or their sweetness forth;<br />\nAnd like her too &#8212; dying beneath neglect.</p></blockquote>\n<p>Cassey would maintain and expand the album, now found in the collection of <a href=\"https://digital.librarycompany.org/islandora/object/Islandora%3ACASS1\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">The Library Company of Philadelphia</a>, for two decades, drawing contributions &#8212; poetry, prose, paintings, sketches &#8212; by some of the era&#8217;s most prominent abolitionists, including Garrison, Lucy Stone, and Frederick Douglass himself. The flowers Sarah Mapps Douglass painted in it are considered the first surviving artworks signed by an African-American woman. </p>\n<figure id=\"attachment_73689\"  class=\"wp-caption aligncenter\"><a href=\"https://society6.com/product/butterfly-by-sarah-mapps-douglass-1833_print?curator=brainpicker\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"><img loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/06/sarahmappsdouglass_butterfly.jpg?resize=680%2C842&#038;ssl=1\" alt=\"\" width=\"680\" height=\"842\" class=\"size-full wp-image-73689\" srcset=\"https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/06/sarahmappsdouglass_butterfly.jpg?w=1200&#38;ssl=1 1200w, https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/06/sarahmappsdouglass_butterfly.jpg?resize=320%2C396&#38;ssl=1 320w, https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/06/sarahmappsdouglass_butterfly.jpg?resize=600%2C743&#38;ssl=1 600w, https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/06/sarahmappsdouglass_butterfly.jpg?resize=240%2C297&#38;ssl=1 240w, https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/06/sarahmappsdouglass_butterfly.jpg?resize=768%2C950&#38;ssl=1 768w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 680px) 100vw, 680px\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></a><figcaption class=\"wp-caption-text\"><em>A token of love, from me, to thee</em> by Sarah Mapps Douglass (Available as <a href=\"https://society6.com/product/butterfly-by-sarah-mapps-douglass-1833_print?curator=brainpicker\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">a print</a> and as <a href=\"https://society6.com/brainpicker/collection/vintage-science-face-masks?curator=brainpicker\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">a face mask</a>.)</figcaption></figure>\n<p>To a similar album complied by one of her pupils, Mary Anne Dickerson, Sarah Mapps Douglass contributed a single, splendid painting of fuchsia &#8212; <em>Fuchsia triphylla</em>, so named in the last years of the 17th century by a French monk and botanist, after a German botanist and herbalist born in the first year of the 16th century &#8212; an uncommonly beautiful and fragrant flowering plant uprooted from its native habitat in Central and South America, so that an uncommonly gifted young woman of uprooted roots could tend to it and paint it in her native antebellum Philadelphia &#8212; a tender reminder that by whatever ugly and unchosen forces we might come into this world, into the dispensation of chance contouring our lives, there is always the choice to consecrate those lives and this world with the willful brush of beauty. </p>\n<figure id=\"attachment_73687\"  class=\"wp-caption aligncenter\"><a href=\"https://society6.com/product/fuchsia-by-sarah-mapps-douglass_print?curator=brainpicker\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"><img loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/06/sarahmappsdouglass_fuchsia.jpg?resize=680%2C869&#038;ssl=1\" alt=\"\" width=\"680\" height=\"869\" class=\"size-full wp-image-73687\" srcset=\"https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/06/sarahmappsdouglass_fuchsia.jpg?w=1124&#38;ssl=1 1124w, https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/06/sarahmappsdouglass_fuchsia.jpg?resize=320%2C409&#38;ssl=1 320w, https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/06/sarahmappsdouglass_fuchsia.jpg?resize=600%2C767&#38;ssl=1 600w, https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/06/sarahmappsdouglass_fuchsia.jpg?resize=240%2C307&#38;ssl=1 240w, https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/06/sarahmappsdouglass_fuchsia.jpg?resize=768%2C982&#38;ssl=1 768w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 680px) 100vw, 680px\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></a><figcaption class=\"wp-caption-text\">Fuchsia by Sarah Mapps Douglass (<a href=\"http://librarycompany.org\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">Library Company of Philadelphia</a>. Available as <a href=\"https://society6.com/product/fuchsia-by-sarah-mapps-douglass_print?curator=brainpicker\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">a print</a> and as <a href=\"https://society6.com/brainpicker/collection/vintage-science-face-masks?curator=brainpicker\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">a face mask</a>.)</figcaption></figure>\n<p>Complement with Frederick Douglass on how photography, <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2019/04/03/virginia-woolf-julia-margaret-cameron-photography/\">improbably invented</a> seven years after Sarah painted her flowers, helped the cause of social justice by <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2017/09/07/frederick-douglass-pictures-and-progress/\">bridging the real and the ideal</a>, then revisit these <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2019/03/12/sarah-stone-natural-history-illustration/\">stunning 18th-century natural history paintings of exotic, endangered, and extinct animals</a> by another trailblazing Sarah of an earlier era and this <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2020/01/29/elizabeth-blackwell-curious-herbal/\">pictorial encyclopedia of medicinal plants</a> a young Scottish mother illustrated another generation earlier to bail her husband out of debtor&#8217;s prison. </p>\n<hr /><h3>donating = loving</h3><p class=\"flipboard-keep\">For 15 years, I have been spending hundreds of hours and thousands of dollars each month to keep <em>Brain Pickings</em> going. It has remained free and ad-free and alive thanks to patronage from readers. I have no staff, no interns, no assistant — a thoroughly one-woman labor of love that is also my life and my livelihood. If this labor makes your life more livable in any way, please consider aiding its sustenance with <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/donate/\">donation</a>.</p>\n\n<hr />\n\n<h3>newsletter</h3>\n<p>Brain Pickings has a free weekly newsletter. 02:35:13","updatedTime":"2021-07-13 02:35:13","title":"Probable Impossibilities: Physicist Alan Lightman on Beginnings, Endings, and What Makes Life Worth Living","link":"https://www.brainpickings.org/?p=73663","description":"<h3>How our cosmic improbability confers dignity and meaning upon our shared existence.</h3>\n<hr>\n<p><a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/gp/product/152474901X/?tag=braipick-20\" target=\"_blank\"><img width=\"320\" height=\"436\" src=\"https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/06/probableimpossibilities_lightman.jpg?fit=320%2C436&#38;ssl=1\" class=\"cover alignright size-medium\" alt=\"Probable Impossibilities: Physicist Alan Lightman on Beginnings, Endings, and What Makes Life Worth Living\" loading=\"lazy\" srcset=\"https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/06/probableimpossibilities_lightman.jpg?w=1650&#38;ssl=1 1650w, https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/06/probableimpossibilities_lightman.jpg?resize=320%2C436&#38;ssl=1 320w, https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/06/probableimpossibilities_lightman.jpg?resize=600%2C818&#38;ssl=1 600w, https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/06/probableimpossibilities_lightman.jpg?resize=240%2C327&#38;ssl=1 240w, https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/06/probableimpossibilities_lightman.jpg?resize=768%2C1047&#38;ssl=1 768w, https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/06/probableimpossibilities_lightman.jpg?resize=1126%2C1536&#38;ssl=1 1126w, https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/06/probableimpossibilities_lightman.jpg?resize=1502%2C2048&#38;ssl=1 1502w, https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/06/probableimpossibilities_lightman.jpg?w=1360&#38;ssl=1 1360w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px\" /></a></p><p>“What exists, exists so that it can be lost and become precious,” Lisel Mueller, who lived to nearly 100, wrote in her gorgeous poem <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2020/02/24/immortality-in-passing-lisel-mueller/\">&#8220;Immortality&#8221;</a> a century and a half after a young artist pointed the world&#8217;s largest telescope at the cosmos to capture <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2019/09/10/john-adams-whipple-moon/\">the first surviving photograph of the Moon</a> and the first-ever photograph of a star: Vega &#8212; an emissary of spacetime, reaching its rays across twenty-five lightyears to imprint the photographic plate with a image of the star as it had been twenty-five years earlier, immortalizing a moment already long gone. </p>\n<p>And yet in a cosmological sense, what exists is precious not because it will one day be lost but because it has overcome the staggering odds of never having existed at all: Within the fraction of matter in the universe that is not dark matter, a fraction of atoms cohered into the elements necessary to form the complex structures necessary for life, of which a tiny portion cohered into the seething cauldron of complexity we call consciousness &#8212; the tiny, improbable fraction of a fraction of a fraction with which we have the perishable privilege of contemplating the universe in our poetry and our physics. </p>\n<p>In <a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/gp/product/152474901X/?tag=braipick-20\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"><strong><em>Probable Impossibilities: Musings on Beginnings and Endings</em></strong></a> (<a href=\"https://www.worldcat.org/title/probable-impossibilities-musings-on-beginnings-and-endings/oclc/1161997924&#038;referer=brief_results\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"><em>public library</em></a>), the poetic physicist <strong>Alan Lightman</strong> sieves four centuries of scientific breakthroughs, from Kepler&#8217;s revolutionary laws of planetary motion to the thousands of habitable exoplanets discovered by NASA&#8217;s <em>Kepler</em> mission, to estimate that even with habitable planets orbiting one tenth of all stars, the faction of living matter in the universe is about one-billionth of one-billionth: If all the matter in the universe were the Gobi desert, life would be but a single grain of sand. </p>\n<figure  class=\"wp-caption alignnone\"><a href=\"http://www.themorgan.org/exhibitions/exhibition.asp?id=90\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"><img src=\"https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/01/thelittleprince_morgan7.jpg?w=680&#038;ssl=1\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></a><figcaption class=\"wp-caption-text\">One of Antoine de Saint-Exupéry&#8217;s <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2014/02/03/exupery-little-prince-morgan-drawings/\">original watercolors for <em>The Little Prince</em></a></figcaption></figure>\n<p>Along the way, Lightman draws delicate lines of figuring from Hindu cosmology to quantum gravity, from Pascal to inflation theory, from Lucretius to Henrietta Leavitt and Edwin Hubble &#8212; lines contouring the most elemental questions that have always animated humanity, questions that are themselves the answer to what it means to be human.</p>\n<p>Building on his lifelong passion for harmonizing our touching human partialities with the fundamental reality of an impartial universe &#8212; our <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2018/03/27/alan-lightman-searching-for-stars-on-an-island-in-maine/\">hunger for absolutes in a relative world</a>, our <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2014/05/22/alan-lightman-accidental-universe-impermanence/\">yearning for permanence in a universe of constant change</a> &#8212; he writes:</p>\n<blockquote><p>As we have struggled through the ages to fathom this strange and wondrous cosmos in which we find ourselves, few ideas have been richer than the concept of nothingness. For to understand anything, as Aristotle argued, we must understand what it is not. To understand matter, said the ancient Greeks, we must understand the “void,” or the absence of matter.</p></blockquote>\n<p>Because we are self-referential creatures &#8212; the consequence of being creatures with selves, itself the consequence of consciousness and the ceaseless electrical storm of neural firings that gives rise to our sense of self &#8212; no void troubles us more than that of our own mortality: the notion of our absence from the scene of life. It is difficult enough to grasp how somethingness could have arisen from nothingness &#8212; how the universe can exist at all. It savages the mind and its animating selfhood to consider that everything &#8212; including the subset constituting the particular something of us &#8212; could dissolve to nothingness. </p>\n<figure  class=\"wp-caption alignnone\"><a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2017/02/10/song-of-two-worlds-alan-lightman-derek-dominic-dsouza/\"><img src=\"https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2017/02/songoftwoworlds3.jpg\" /></a><figcaption class=\"wp-caption-text\">Art by Derek Dominic D’souza from <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2017/02/10/song-of-two-worlds-alan-lightman-derek-dominic-dsouza/\"><em>Song of Two Worlds</em></a> by Alan Lightman</figcaption></figure>\n<p>It is a discomposing notion &#8212; even for a physicist without delusion about the materiality of life, with a soulful reverence for the poetry of existence. Lightman closes his essay on the science of nothingness with a sentiment of touching, inescapable humanity:</p>\n<blockquote><p>What I feel and I know is that I am here now, at this moment in the grand sweep of time. I am not part of the void. I am not a fluctuation in the quantum vacuum. Even though I understand that someday my atoms will be scattered in soil and in air, that I will no longer exist, I am alive now. I am feeling this moment. I can see my hand on my writing desk. I can feel the warmth of the Sun through the window. And looking out, I can see a pine-needled path that goes down to the sea.</p></blockquote>\n<p>Another essay, titled &#8220;Immortality,&#8221; explores this irreconcilable dissonance between the creaturely and the cosmic &#8212; the dissonance from which we make our most symphonic art as we try to fathom our existence. Lying in his hammock one summer day, Lightman observes:</p>\n<blockquote><p>A hundred years from now, I’ll be gone, but many of these spruce and cedars will still be here. The wind going through them will still sound like a distant waterfall. The curve of the land will be the same as it is now. The paths that I wander may still be here, although probably covered with new vegetation. The rocks and ledges on the shore will be here, including a particular ledge I’m quite fond of, shaped like the knuckled back of a large animal. Sometimes, I sit on that ledge and wonder if it will remember me. Even my house might still be here, or at least the concrete posts of its footing, crumbling in the salt air. But eventually, of course, even this land will shift and change and dissolve. Nothing persists in the material world. All of it changes and passes away.</p></blockquote>\n<figure id=\"attachment_73675\"  class=\"wp-caption aligncenter\"><a href=\"https://society6.com/product/non-space-by-robert-fludd-1617_print?sku=s6-20687244p4a1v46?curator=brainpicker\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"><img loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/06/robertfludd_vacuum1.jpg?resize=680%2C672&#038;ssl=1\" alt=\"\" width=\"680\" height=\"672\" class=\"size-full wp-image-73675\" srcset=\"https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/06/robertfludd_vacuum1.jpg?w=1200&#38;ssl=1 1200w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/06/robertfludd_vacuum1.jpg?resize=320%2C316&#38;ssl=1 320w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/06/robertfludd_vacuum1.jpg?resize=600%2C593&#38;ssl=1 600w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/06/robertfludd_vacuum1.jpg?resize=240%2C237&#38;ssl=1 240w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/06/robertfludd_vacuum1.jpg?resize=768%2C758&#38;ssl=1 768w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 680px) 100vw, 680px\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></a><figcaption class=\"wp-caption-text\">Robert Fludd&#8217;s pioneering 1617 conception of non-space, long before the notion of the vacuum existed in cosmology. (Available as <a href=\"https://society6.com/product/non-space-by-robert-fludd-1617_print?sku=s6-20687244p4a1v46?curator=brainpicker\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">a print</a> and as <a href=\"https://society6.com/brainpicker/collection/vintage-science-face-masks?curator=brainpicker\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">a face mask</a>.)</figcaption></figure>\n<p>And yet, in an echo of one of the book&#8217;s subtlest yet profoundest undertones, Lightman challenges our binary view of life and death. With an eye to consciousness &#8212; &#8220;the seemingly strange experience&#8221; that furnishes &#8220;the most profound and troubling aspect of human existence&#8221; &#8212; he argues that death is not the life-switch in the off position but the gradual dimming of consciousness, of our experience of aliveness, through the deterioration of its physical infrastructure. </p>\n<p>Ever since Cecilia Payne <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2019/05/08/cecilia-payne-harvard-observatory-radio-talks/\">discovered the chemical fingerprint of the universe</a>, we have known that the atoms we are made of &#8212; seven thousand trillion trillion atoms in each of us, on average &#8212; were forged in the furnace of faraway stars. We know, too, that every cell in our bodies &#8212; the tendons that stiffen our fists and the cortices that kindle our tenderness &#8212; is made of atoms. Lightman writes:</p>\n<blockquote><p>To an alien intelligence, each of us human beings would appear to be an assemblage of atoms, humming with our various electrical and chemical energies. To be sure, it is a special assemblage. A rock does not behave like a person&#8230; When we die, this special assemblage disassembles. The atoms remain, only scattered about.</p></blockquote>\n<figure id=\"attachment_72916\"  class=\"wp-caption aligncenter\"><a href=\"https://society6.com/product/art-by-dorothy-lathrop-for-down-adown-derry-by-walter-de-la-mare-19224642772_print?curator=brainpicker\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"><img loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/downadownderry_dorothylathrop17.jpg?resize=680%2C801&#038;ssl=1\" alt=\"\" width=\"680\" height=\"801\" class=\"size-full wp-image-72916\" srcset=\"https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/downadownderry_dorothylathrop17.jpg?w=1200&#38;ssl=1 1200w, https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/downadownderry_dorothylathrop17.jpg?resize=320%2C377&#38;ssl=1 320w, https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/downadownderry_dorothylathrop17.jpg?resize=600%2C707&#38;ssl=1 600w, https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/downadownderry_dorothylathrop17.jpg?resize=240%2C283&#38;ssl=1 240w, https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/downadownderry_dorothylathrop17.jpg?resize=768%2C905&#38;ssl=1 768w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 680px) 100vw, 680px\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></a><figcaption class=\"wp-caption-text\">Art by <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2021/03/09/dorothy-lathrop-down-adown-derry/\">Dorothy Lathrop</a>, 1922. (Available <a href=\"https://society6.com/product/art-by-dorothy-lathrop-for-down-adown-derry-by-walter-de-la-mare-19224642772_print?curator=brainpicker\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">as a print</a>.)</figcaption></figure>\n<p>That special assemblage is what we call consciousness. A century after Virginia Woolf observed that <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2015/02/06/virginia-woolf-soul-aging/\">“one can’t write directly about the soul [for] looked at, it vanishes,”</a> Lightman writes:</p>\n<blockquote><p>The soul, as commonly understood, we cannot discuss scientifically. Not so with consciousness, and the closely related Self. Isn’t the experience of consciousness and Self an illusion caused by those trillions of neuronal connections and electrical and chemical flows? If you don’t like the word <em>illusion</em>, then you can stick with the sensation itself. You can say that what we call the Self is a name we give to the mental sensation of certain electrical and chemical flows in our neurons. That sensation is rooted in the material brain. And I do not mean to diminish the brain in any way by affirming its materiality. The human brain is capable of all of the wondrous feats of imagination and self-reflection and thought that we ascribe to our highest existence. But I do claim that it’s all atoms and molecules. If the alien intelligence examined a human being in detail, he/she/it would see fluids flowing, sodium and potassium gates opening and closing as electricity races through nerve cells, acetylcholine molecules migrating between synapses. But he/she/it would not find a Self. The Self and consciousness, I think, are names we give to the sensations produced by all of those electrical and chemical flows.</p>\n<p>If someone began disassembling my brain one neuron at a time, depending on where the process began I might first lose a few motor skills, then some memories, then perhaps the ability to find particular words to make sentences, the ability to recognize faces, the ability to know where I was. During this slow taking apart of my brain, I would become more and more disoriented. Everything I associate with my ego and Self would gradually dissolve away into a bog of confusion and minimal existence. The doctors in their blue and green scrub suits could drop the removed neurons, one by one, into a metal bowl. Each a tiny gray gelatinous blob. Stringy with the axons and dendrites. Soft, so you would not hear the little thuds as each plopped in the bowl.</p></blockquote>\n<p>An understanding of death as &#8220;the name that we give to a collection of atoms that once had the special arrangement of a functioning neuronal network and now no longer does so&#8221; renders the boundary between life and death more like a shoreline redrawn by the receding tide pool than like a coastal cliff dropping off into the abyss. And yet even as a scientific materialist with no mystical inclinations and no belief in an afterlife, Lightman remains what we all are &#8212; fundamentally human in our special assemblage of atoms &#8212; and gives voice to that fundamental humanity with uncommon splendor of sentiment: </p>\n<blockquote><p>Despite my belief that I am only a collection of atoms, that my awareness is passing away neuron by neuron, I am content with the illusion of consciousness. I’ll take it. And I find a pleasure in knowing that a hundred years from now, even a thousand years from now, some of my atoms will remain in this place where I now lie in my hammock. Those atoms will not know where they came from, but they will have been mine. Some of them will once have been part of the memory of my mother dancing the bossa nova. Some will once have been part of the memory of the vinegary smell of my first apartment. Some will once have been part of my hand. If I could label each of my atoms at this moment, imprint each with my Social Security number, someone could follow them for the next thousand years as they floated in air, mixed with the soil, became parts of particular plants and trees, dissolved in the ocean and then floated again to the air. Some will undoubtedly become parts of other people, particular people. Some will become parts of other lives, other memories. That might be a kind of immortality.</p></blockquote>\n<figure id=\"attachment_72737\"  class=\"wp-caption aligncenter\"><a href=\"https://society6.com/product/art-from-thomas-wrights-an-original-theory-or-new-hypothesis-of-the-universe-1750_print?curator=brainpicker\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"><img loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/02/thomaswright_galaxies3.jpg?resize=680%2C977&#038;ssl=1\" alt=\"\" width=\"680\" height=\"977\" class=\"size-full wp-image-72737\" srcset=\"https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/02/thomaswright_galaxies3.jpg?w=1200&#38;ssl=1 1200w, https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/02/thomaswright_galaxies3.jpg?resize=240%2C345&#38;ssl=1 240w, https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/02/thomaswright_galaxies3.jpg?resize=320%2C460&#38;ssl=1 320w, https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/02/thomaswright_galaxies3.jpg?resize=768%2C1103&#38;ssl=1 768w, https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/02/thomaswright_galaxies3.jpg?resize=600%2C862&#38;ssl=1 600w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 680px) 100vw, 680px\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></a><figcaption class=\"wp-caption-text\">Art from Thomas Wright&#8217;s <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2021/02/16/thomas-wright-original-theory/\"><em>An Original Theory or New Hypothesis of the Universe</em></a>, 1750 &#8212; the first book to describe the spiral shape of the Milky Way. (Available as <a href=\"https://society6.com/product/art-from-thomas-wrights-an-original-theory-or-new-hypothesis-of-the-universe-1750_print?curator=brainpicker\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">a print</a> and as <a href=\"https://society6.com/brainpicker/collection/vintage-science-face-masks?curator=brainpicker\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">a face mask</a>.)</figcaption></figure>\n<p>As if it were not staggering enough how tiny a fraction of space life animates, Lightman observes that it also animates a fraction of time &#8212; not merely in terms of the transience of any one life, but in terms of all life occupying only a slender slice of the totality of time in the universe, as the discovery of cosmic acceleration has revealed. The cosmic brevity of &#8220;the era of life&#8221; is bookended on one end by the slow condensation of colossal gas clouds into the first stars that forged the first atoms large enough to form complex structures, after the universe had already existed for about one billion years, and bookended on the other by the eventual death of all stars when they burn out in several thousand billion years, leaving behind a dark lifeless expanse of pure spacetime. </p>\n<p>Here we each are, each existence a summer day suspended in the hammock of spacetime.</p>\n<p>And yet even in these cold unfeeling cosmic facts, Lightman finds reason to swell the brevity of existence with the warm feeling of kinship that makes life worth living. With an eye to his grain-of-Gobi-sand analogy, he writes:</p>\n<blockquote><p>Life in our universe is a flash in the pan, a few moments in the vast unfolding of time and space in the cosmos&#8230; A realization of the scarcity of life makes me feel some ineffable connection to other living things&#8230; a kinship in being among those few grains of sand in the desert, or present during the relatively brief era of life in the vast temporal sprawl of the universe.</p>\n<p>[&#8230;]</p>\n<p>We share something in the vast corridors of this cosmos we find ourselves in. What exactly is it we share? Certainly, the mundane attributes of “life”: the ability to separate ourselves from our surroundings, to utilize energy sources, to grow, to reproduce, to evolve. I would argue that we “conscious” beings share something more during our relatively brief moment in the “era of life”: the ability to witness and reflect on the spectacle of existence, a spectacle that is at once mysterious, joyous, tragic, trembling, majestic, confusing, comic, nurturing, unpredictable and predictable, ecstatic, beautiful, cruel, sacred, devastating, exhilarating. The cosmos will grind on for eternity long after we’re gone, cold and unobserved. But for these few powers of ten, we have been. We have seen, we have felt, we have lived.</p></blockquote>\n<figure id=\"attachment_68575\"  class=\"wp-caption aligncenter\"><a href=\"https://society6.com/product/light-distribution-on-soap-bubble-from-le-monde-physique-1882_print?sku=s6-11475521p4a1v1?curator=brainpicker\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"><img loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2019/08/4.jpg?resize=680%2C993&#038;ssl=1\" alt=\"\" width=\"680\" height=\"993\" class=\"size-full wp-image-68575\" srcset=\"https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2019/08/4.jpg?w=2197&#38;ssl=1 2197w, https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2019/08/4.jpg?resize=240%2C351&#38;ssl=1 240w, https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2019/08/4.jpg?resize=320%2C467&#38;ssl=1 320w, https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2019/08/4.jpg?resize=768%2C1122&#38;ssl=1 768w, https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2019/08/4.jpg?resize=600%2C876&#38;ssl=1 600w, https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2019/08/4.jpg?w=1360&#38;ssl=1 1360w, https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2019/08/4.jpg?w=2040&#38;ssl=1 2040w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 680px) 100vw, 680px\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></a><figcaption class=\"wp-caption-text\">Light distribution on soap bubble from <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2019/08/20/amedee-guillemin-le-monde-physique/\"><em>Les phénomènes de la physique</em></a> by Amédée Guillemin, 1882. (Available <a href=\"https://society6.com/product/light-distribution-on-soap-bubble-from-le-monde-physique-1882_print?sku=s6-11475521p4a1v1?curator=brainpicker\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">as a print</a> and <a href=\"https://society6.com/brainpicker/collection/vintage-science-face-masks?curator=brainpicker\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">as a face mask</a>.)</figcaption></figure>\n<p>Complement these fragments of Lightman&#8217;s altogether luminous <a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/gp/product/152474901X/?tag=braipick-20\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"><strong><em>Probable Impossibilities</em></strong></a> with physicist Brian Greene&#8217;s Rilke-inspired reflection on <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2020/12/25/brian-greene-rilke/\">how to fill our transience with maximal aliveness</a> and Maya Angelou&#8217;s Sagan-inspired <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2018/05/09/a-brave-and-startling-truth-maya-angelou/\">ode to the cosmic specialness of our humanity</a>, then revisit a Borges-inspired meditation on <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2021/06/10/the-mirror-of-enigmas-borges-bloy/\">chance, the universe, and what makes us who we are</a>.</p>\n<hr /><h3>donating = loving</h3><p class=\"flipboard-keep\">For 15 years, I have been spending hundreds of hours and thousands of dollars each month to keep <em>Brain Pickings</em> going. It has remained free and ad-free and alive thanks to patronage from readers. I have no staff, no interns, no assistant — a thoroughly one-woman labor of love that is also my life and my livelihood. 02:35:13","updatedTime":"2021-07-13 02:35:13","title":"The Creative Urge: John Coltrane on Perseverance Against Rejection, the Innovator’s Mindset, and How Hardship Fuels Art","link":"https://www.brainpickings.org/?p=73840","description":"<h3>&#8220;Innovators always seek to revitalize, extend and reconstruct the status quo in their given fields&#8230; Quite often they are the rejects, outcasts, sub-citizens, etc. of the very societies to which they bring so much sustenance.&#8221;</h3>\n<hr>\n<p><a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Coltrane-Biography-Cuthbert-Ormond-Simpkins/dp/0933121202/?tag=braipick-20\" target=\"_blank\"><img width=\"320\" height=\"501\" src=\"https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/coltrane_simpkins.jpg?fit=320%2C501&#38;ssl=1\" class=\"cover alignright size-medium\" alt=\"The Creative Urge: John Coltrane on Perseverance Against Rejection, the Innovator&#8217;s Mindset, and How Hardship Fuels Art\" loading=\"lazy\" srcset=\"https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/coltrane_simpkins.jpg?w=1200&#38;ssl=1 1200w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/coltrane_simpkins.jpg?resize=320%2C501&#38;ssl=1 320w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/coltrane_simpkins.jpg?resize=600%2C940&#38;ssl=1 600w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/coltrane_simpkins.jpg?resize=240%2C376&#38;ssl=1 240w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/coltrane_simpkins.jpg?resize=768%2C1203&#38;ssl=1 768w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/coltrane_simpkins.jpg?resize=980%2C1536&#38;ssl=1 980w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px\" /></a></p><p>To create anything of beauty, daring, and substance that makes the world see itself afresh &#8212; be it a <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2019/12/26/katharina-kepler-witchcraft-dream/\">revolutionary law of planetary motion</a> or the <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2014/11/13/van-gogh-starry-night-fluid-dynamics-animation/\"><em>Starry Night</em></a> &#8212; is the work of lonely persistence against the tides of convention and conformity, often at the cost of the visionary&#8217;s aching ostracism from the status quo they are challenging with their vision. Rilke recognized this when he observed that <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2018/06/22/rilke-patience-solitude-art/\">&#8220;works of art are of an infinite loneliness&#8221;</a> and Baldwin recognized it in his  <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2014/08/20/james-baldwin-the-creative-process/\">classic investigation of the creative process</a>, in which he argued that the primary distinction of the artist is the willingness to maintain the state everyone else most zealously avoids: aloneness &#8212; not the romantic solitude of the hermit by the silver stream, but the raw existential and creative loneliness Baldwin likened to &#8220;the aloneness of birth or death&#8221; or &#8220;the aloneness of love, the force and mystery that so many have extolled and so many have cursed, but which no one has ever understood or ever really been able to control.&#8221; </p>\n<p>This love-like force &#8212; the creative force &#8212; is what fuels the perseverance necessary to usher in a new way of seeing or a new way of being. It is the life-force by which visionaries survive the aloneness of their countercultural lives.</p>\n<p>That is what jazz legend <strong>John Coltrane</strong> (September 23, 1926&#8211;July 17, 1967) addressed in an extraordinary letter penned in the late spring of 1962, posthumously included in Cuthbert Ormond Simpkins&#8217;s excellent 1975 biography <a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Coltrane-Biography-Cuthbert-Ormond-Simpkins/dp/0933121202/?tag=braipick-20\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"><strong><em>Coltrane</em></strong></a> (<a href=\"https://www.worldcat.org/title/coltrane-a-biography/oclc/19749872?referer=br&#038;ht=edition\" target=\"_blabnk\" rel=\"noopener\"><em>public library</em></a>).</p>\n<figure id=\"attachment_73841\"  class=\"wp-caption aligncenter\"><img loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/johncoltrane.jpg?resize=680%2C487&#038;ssl=1\" alt=\"\" width=\"680\" height=\"487\" class=\"size-full wp-image-73841\" srcset=\"https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/johncoltrane.jpg?w=1968&#38;ssl=1 1968w, https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/johncoltrane.jpg?resize=320%2C229&#38;ssl=1 320w, https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/johncoltrane.jpg?resize=600%2C429&#38;ssl=1 600w, https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/johncoltrane.jpg?resize=240%2C172&#38;ssl=1 240w, https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/johncoltrane.jpg?resize=768%2C549&#38;ssl=1 768w, https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/johncoltrane.jpg?resize=1536%2C1099&#38;ssl=1 1536w, https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/johncoltrane.jpg?w=1360&#38;ssl=1 1360w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 680px) 100vw, 680px\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /><figcaption class=\"wp-caption-text\">John Coltrane (Courtesy of <a href=\"http://johncoltrane.com\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">johncoltrane.com</a>.)</figcaption></figure>\n<p>One June morning two years after the release of his epoch-making <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2011/01/26/synesthesia-music-visualization/\"><em>Giant Steps</em></a> and five years before his untimely death of cancer, Coltrane opened his mailbox to discover a package from the editor of <em>Downbeat</em> magazine, the premier journal of jazz, containing a gift: a copy of <a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Music-Imagination-Aaron-Copland/dp/B001JPF9KG/?tag=braipick-20\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"><em>Music and Imagination</em></a> &#8212; a book of the six lectures the great composer and <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2014/09/09/aaron-copland-creative-experience/\">creativity-contemplator</a> Aaron Copland had delivered at Harvard a decade earlier. </p>\n<p>Coltrane&#8217;s letter of thanks for the gift unspools into one of those rare miracles when something small and seemingly peripheral prompts a sweeping yet succinct formulation of a visionary&#8217;s personal philosophy and creative credo &#8212; Coltrane&#8217;s most direct meditation on what it means to be an artist. </p>\n<p>Millennia after <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2021/03/02/pythagoras-sappho-music/\">Pythagoras&#8217;s revolutionary yet limited mathematics of music</a> forked the sonic path of the modern world by laying the structural foundation of the Western canon but failing to account for the intricate unstructured musical styles of the African diaspora and my own native Balkans &#8212; a cultural irony, given Pythagoras developed his theory on the island of Samos, a thriving cross-pollinator of the Ancient Greek world perched midway between Africa and the Balkans &#8212; Coltrane observes that Copland&#8217;s lectures, while erudite and philosophically insightful, speak more to musicians in the Western tradition than they do to jazz musicians. Against Copland&#8217;s concern about how difficult it can be for artists to find &#8220;a positive philosophy or justification&#8221; for their art, Coltrane holds up jazz as living counterpoint &#8212; a musical tradition that began as an affirmation of life amid unimaginable hardship, provided a lifeline for those who conceived it and partook of it, and has thrived on the wings of this inherent buoyancy. </p>\n<figure  class=\"wp-caption alignnone\"><a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2011/07/25/childrens-books-by-adult-authors-2/#langstonhughes\"><img src=\"https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2011/07/firstbookofjazz_hughes1.jpg\" /></a><figcaption class=\"wp-caption-text\">Art from <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2011/07/25/childrens-books-by-adult-authors-2/#langstonhughes\"><em>The First Book of Jazz</em></a> by Langston Hughes, 1954</figcaption></figure>\n<p>He writes: </p>\n<blockquote><p>It is really easy for us [jazz musicians] to create. We are born with this feeling that just comes out no matter what conditions exist. Otherwise, how could our founding fathers have produced this music in the first place when they surely found themselves (as many of us do today) existing in hostile communities when there was everything to fear and damn few to trust. Any music which could grow and propagate itself as our music has, must have a hell of an affirmative belief inherent in it.</p></blockquote>\n<p>Since we read (and write) about other lives to make sense of our own, he reflects on a biography he has been reading of Van Gogh &#8212; an artist who spent his short, revolutionary, tragic life negotiating between <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2017/08/23/gauguin-van-gogh-ear/\">his private suffering</a> and <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2014/12/01/van-gogh-purpose-letter/\">the irrepressible affirmative belief</a> that forever changed art. With an eye to Van Gogh, Coltrane writes:</p>\n<blockquote><p>Truth is indestructible&#8230; History shows (and it’s the same way today) that the innovator is more often than not met with some degree of condemnation; usually according to the degree of his departure from the prevailing modes of expression or what have you. Change is always so hard to accept.</p></blockquote>\n<figure id=\"attachment_73842\"  class=\"wp-caption aligncenter\"><img loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/vangogh_mulberry.jpg?resize=680%2C567&#038;ssl=1\" alt=\"\" width=\"680\" height=\"567\" class=\"size-full wp-image-73842\" srcset=\"https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/vangogh_mulberry.jpg?w=1077&#38;ssl=1 1077w, https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/vangogh_mulberry.jpg?resize=320%2C267&#38;ssl=1 320w, https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/vangogh_mulberry.jpg?resize=600%2C500&#38;ssl=1 600w, https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/vangogh_mulberry.jpg?resize=240%2C200&#38;ssl=1 240w, https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/vangogh_mulberry.jpg?resize=768%2C640&#38;ssl=1 768w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 680px) 100vw, 680px\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /><figcaption class=\"wp-caption-text\">Vincent van Gogh: <em>The Mulberry Tree</em>, 1889.</figcaption></figure>\n<p>In a sentiment evocative of artist Egon Schiele&#8217;s observation that <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2018/06/01/egon-schiele-letter/\">visionaries tend to come from the minority</a> and echoing the seventh of Bertrand Russell&#8217;s <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2012/05/02/a-liberal-decalogue-bertrand-russell/\">ten commandments of critical thinking</a> &#8212; <em>“Do not fear to be eccentric in opinion, for every opinion now accepted was once eccentric.”</em> &#8212; Coltrane adds:</p>\n<blockquote><p>Innovators always seek to revitalize, extend and reconstruct the status quo in their given fields, wherever it is needed. Quite often they are the rejects, outcasts, sub-citizens, etc. of the very societies to which they bring so much sustenance. Often they are people who endure great personal tragedy in their lives. Whatever the case, whether accepted or rejected, rich or poor, they are forever guided by that great and eternal constant &#8212; the creative urge.</p></blockquote>\n<p>This might be the most succinct summation of my creative choice of historical figures to celebrate in <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2018/11/01/figuring/\"><em>Figuring</em></a>. It is also what Virginia Woolf meant when she wrote of the <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2015/09/09/virginia-woolf-cotton-wool-moments-of-being/\">&#8220;shock-receiving capacity&#8221;</a> necessary for being an artist, and what Patti Smith meant when, with an eye to Coltrane, she considered <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2016/11/22/more-songwriters-on-songwriting-patti-smith-interview/\">the shamanistic channeling</a> at the heart of the creative impulse. </p>\n<p>Complement with Coltrane&#8217;s contemporary and fellow jazz legend Bill Evans on <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2014/10/30/the-universal-mind-of-bill-evans/\">the creative process</a>, then revisit Walt Whitman on <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2019/01/03/whitman-emerson-criticism/\">how to keep criticism from sinking your creative confidence</a>.</p>\n<hr /><h3>donating = loving</h3><p class=\"flipboard-keep\">For 15 years, I have been spending hundreds of hours and thousands of dollars each month to keep <em>Brain Pickings</em> going. It has remained free and ad-free and alive thanks to patronage from readers. I have no staff, no interns, no assistant — a thoroughly one-woman labor of love that is also my life and my livelihood. If this labor makes your life more livable in any way, please consider aiding its sustenance with <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/donate/\">donation</a>.</p>\n\n<hr />\n\n<h3>newsletter</h3>\n<p>Brain Pickings has a free weekly newsletter.
